Approval
This thesis has been prepared over six months at the Section for Computational Atomic-scale Materials
Design, Department of Physics, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfilment
for the degree Master of Science in Engineering, MSc. Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has basic knowledge in quantum mechanics, statistical physics and
condensed matter theory.
A background in magnetism or ab-initio simulations is helpful but not
assumed.
Frederik L. Durhuus - s163969

ii
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Magnetic order in 2D materials has become a highly active research field, since the first fabrication
of 2D magnets in 2017, with promising applications, especially in spintronics. The main hindrance to
progress is that the critical, ordering temperature is low in all fabricated 2D magnets. To work towards
creating new high temperature 2D magnets, I gathered theory on first principle simulations of critical
temperatures in magnets, and developed a computational scheme for simulating said temperatures. In
particular, I focused on the itinerant magnetism found in metals, which is poorly understood due to
the complex interplay of localised and delocalised states. Because the critical temperature is largely
determined by spin-wave fluctuations in the ordered ground state, the computational scheme largely
deals with how to simulate the spectrum of spin-wave excitations. This is done with the well-known
Heisenberg model, but using a novel approach for extracting the exchange parameters from DFT
simulations. An approach which relies on the first ever pure-plane wave implementation of the magnetic
force theorem. I present a detailed derivation of the exchange formula and a convergence study of
my implementation. The computed spin-wave spectra are in excellent agreement with literature and
novelties in the implementation give new ways to study the degree of localisation for magnetically
active electrons.
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets

Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:7==
1
Introduction
Magnets have been a part of human history for ages. The ancient Greeks knew how magnetite lumps
could attract iron fillings without contact[85]. About one and a half millennia later, Chinese and
European scholars independently realised how to make the first artificial, permanent magnets out of
iron, which led to the invention of the compass[11]. Today magnetic materials have found a wide range
of practical applications e.g. in electric motors, medical MRI, power conversion technologies such as
for windmill and water turbines, automated assembly lines in heavy industry and hard drives for data
storage. In fact the global market for magnetic materials was 60 billion euros in 2019, with almost a
doubling projected by 2027[50].
Despite many mature technologies and decades of fundamental research by the likes of Bohr, Heisen-
berg, and Anderson[75], the study of magnetism in condensed matter systems continues to yield new
physical insights and novel technology.
Researchers are actively studying the connection between
magnetic order and high temperature superconductivity[66, 73, 65, 82]. It is predicted that a cer-
tain kind of magnetic state, known as a quantum spin liquid, supports majorana fermions[83], which
are crucial for topological quantum computing[2]. Induction heating of magnetic nanoparticles has
been studied as a future cancer treatment[13] and for temperature control in chemical reactors[33].
Also, much research is going into developing spintronics[28]; information processing by electron spin
e.g. spin-polarised currents, and magnonics[43]; information processing by wave-like collective spin
motion.
Recent progress has been facilitated by advances in experimental and theoretical analysis techniques.
On the theoretical side, improvements in computing power and simulation methods such as Monte
Carlo and Density Functional Theory (DFT) has enabled the ab-initio simulation of realistic materials.
Regarding experiments, large scale X-ray and neutron facilities enable the study of magnetic structure
and excitations in bulk samples, with upcoming facilities such as the European Spallation Source
promising major improvements in resolution[84]. Meanwhile, improvements in nanofabrication and
visualisation techniques has led to renewed interest in low dimensional magnetic systems. For example,
NV-center magnetometry can detect magnetic fields from a single electron spin, and spin-polarised
STM can measure spin configurations in surfaces with atomic precision[31].
A particularly hot topic right now is the study of magnetism in materials that are just a few atoms
thick. Although the first truly two-dimensional (2D) material was isolated in 2004[59], the first 2D
magnet, namely CrI3, was not fabricated until 2017[32]. CrI3 is an insulator, but it was soon followed
by the first 2D metallic magnet[15], Fe3GeTe2. These are both examples of Van der Waals (vdW)
materials, i.e. stacks of planar crystals weakly bonded through vdW forces. Once you have 2D vdW
crystals isolated, you can stack them with other vdW materials, irrespective of lattice matching, thus
forming an extremely broad class of heterostructures, that combine the properties at the atomic level.
Also, you can fine tune these structure by electrical gating, interlayer twist angles, interlayer distances,
mechanical strain, chemical functionalisation etc.
In the long run, such heterostructures promise
unprecedented control and tunability of material properties down to the quantum level, perhaps
ushering in a new age of designer materials. In a shorter time frame, 2D magnets and heterostructures
are interesting both as low-power supplements to existing semiconductor electronics and for whole new
paradigms of data storage and information processing, classical and quantum [19].
As mentioned in several recent review papers[17, 90, 31], a critical next step is to extend the set
of available materials, in particular to find 2D magnets with stable magnetic order under ambient
conditions. Insulating and semiconducting magnets are easier to integrate with the existing semicon-
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
1
==PAGE:8==
ducter electronics, while metallic 2D magnets might have higher ordering temperatures in general, in
analogy to the bulk case. This latter point is critical as 2D magnets are generally less stable than their
bulk counterparts, most, if not all, known 2D magnets disorder near or below room temperature. To
quote To quote Nobel Laureate Albert Fert вАЭThere will be applications, provided the low temperature
problem can be solved.[16]вАЭ1
To further the field of 2D magnetism, the ultimate goal of this thesis is to theoretically predict new
2D magnets with relatively high critical temperatures. To this end, I have worked on a computational
framework for high-throughput predictions of ordering temperatures. High-throughput meaning that
you it is fast and standardised enough to run on hundreds of systems, e.g. those of the Computational
2D Materials Database (C2DB)[24, 18]. To predict the properties of materials that have yet to exist,
I use DFT based ab-initio calculations. These only yield zero-temperature properties, so to predict
critical temperatures, I use model Hamiltonians with numerical parameter values acquired from the
ab-initio results. The critical temperature is mainly determined by the thermodynamics of the low-
energy excitations which destabilise the ordered state. Therefore, a large part of the current project
has been the development of theory and algorithms for the ab initio simulation of these excitations in a
manner compatible with DTU PhysicsвАЩ own open source DFT code, GPAW[57, 14]. Because of major
novelties in the implementation, I performed extensive tests and benchmarking on known systems, in
particular the elementary, bulk ferromagnets iron, cobalt and nickel. I note that the methods are not
restricted to 2D materials or temperature determination, so the potential applications are extremely
broad.
The report is structured as follows. In chapter 2 I present various relevant ideas and background results
such as the explicit connection between critical temperature and low-energy excitations. In chapter 3
I derive an eigenvalue problem for the spin-wave spectrum by a Holstein-Primakoff bosonisation of the
Heisenberg model. In chapter 4 I use perturbation theory on the DFT and Heisenberg Hamiltonians
to derive a pivotal formula, which expresses Heisenberg parameters in terms of quantities that can be
computed ab-initio. The formula is an example of the Magnetic Force Theorem (MFT). In chapter 5
I rewrite the MFT formula from chapter 4 in a form that enables a pure plane-wave implementation,
compatible with GPAW. In chapter 6 I explain the numerical implementation and present an extensive
convergence study to benchmark it. Finally, in chapter 7 I compare my computed magnon spectra
and critical temperatures to literature results. Then discuss limitations, potential improvements and
directions of future study related to this project, before the customary conclusion. Note that the MFT
formula was originally presented in [7], though with almost no intermediate calculations. However my
plane-wave implementation of the formula is entirely novel, hence that implementation constitutes the
main result of the present thesis.

==PAGE:9==
2
Preliminary theory
2.1
Magnets : a microscopic view
Figure 2.1: Sketches of magnetism at different length scales.
The pedestrian view of a magnet is a piece of material, with a north and south pole, that attracts
or repels other magnets through magnetic fields. But what makes some pieces of matter magnetic
and others non-magnetic? In classical electromagnetism[21], one learns that moving charges create
magnetic fields.
When the charges move in circles, you can characterise the magnetic field by a
vector quantity known as a magnetic moment. All matter has some density distribution of magnetic
moments, called magnetisation, m(r), which is typically visualised as a grid of arrows. Even living
tissue can be magnetised if you apply a large enough B-field, as Andre Geim showed by levitating
a frog[74]. Magnetic materials are those for which internal interactions cause the moments to form
ordered structures in equilibrium.
Examples of magnetic order in 1D and 2D are shown in fig. 2.2. In ferromagnets, such as bulk iron, the
moments tend to align so they add up and produce a macroscopic B-field. This is the typical basis of
permanent magnets, but other, subtler patterns also have their uses. For instance, antiferromagnetic
(AFM) order is more stable against external fields, precisely because there is no macroscopic moment,
which makes AFM order useful for data storage[90]. Also shown in fig. 2.2 is an AFM on a triangular
lattice. Here, it is energetically favourable to anti-align, but if 2 spins on a triangle do so, the third
has nowhere to point. This is an example of geometric frustration, which gives a highly degenerate,
non-collinear groundstate. Such frustration is essential in realising quantum spin liquids [72] and may
induce skyrmions[47], i.e. localised vortex structures of magnetic moments.
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
3
==PAGE:10==
Figure 2.2: Examples of magnetic order in 1D and 2D. Ferromagnetism = full alignment, antiferromagnetism =
antialignment, ferrimagnetism = antialignment of inequivalent moments with a net moment and spiral order (green
rings to guide the eye), skyrmion = localised spin vortex. To the right is an antiferromagnet (AFM) on a 2D triangular
lattice. Here antialignment is energetically favourable, but full antialignment is impossible, which is called geometric
frustration. Instead a highly degenerate groundstate with 120вИШbetween neighbours emerge as a compromise. The
spiral, skymion and frustrated AFM are non-collinear states, while the rest are collinear as all moments point along
the same axis.
The natural next question is where the magnetic moments in materials come from. It was shown
by Niels Bohr in his PhD. thesis that classical physics does not permit magnetic order in thermal
equilibrium and time-invariant B-fields[67].
In other words, magnetism as a material property is
inherently quantum. Indeed, the source of equilibrium magnetism is to be found in single electrons,
see fig. 2.1 blue zoom.
In a semi-classical picture, an electron has an orbital magnetic moment,
associated with its circular orbit, and a spin magnetic moment, from the electron spinning around its
own center. The magnetic moment of an electron is[6].
mрЭСТрЭСЩрЭСТрЭСРрЭС°рЭСЯрЭСЬрЭСЫ= вИТ1
вДПрЭЬЗрЭРµ(рЭСФS + L)
,
рЭЬЗрЭРµ= рЭСТвДП
2рЭСЪрЭСТ
= 9.274 √Ч 10вИТ24Am2
Semi-classical picture
Usually, the spin magnetic moment is the main contribution, while orbital contributions are a minor
correction. Therefore, people often refer exclusively to spin when discussing magnetic order. The
current project does not take orbital moments into account at all.
In a more accurate, quantum picture, the orbital moment is quantised along with orbital angular
momentum, spin is an intrinsic particle property on par with charge, and the electron itself is delo-
calised with a cloud-like probability distribution of position and momentum. Because electrons are
spin-1/2 particles, each electron spin can take only 2 discrete values, up or down. Hence you can
describe the magnetisation of a material in terms of a density of up spins, рЭСЫвЖС, and down arrows, рЭСЫвЖУ.
Alternatively, you can use electron density рЭСЫ= рЭСЫвЖС+ рЭСЫвЖУand spin moment, which in a collinear state
is рЭСЪ= рЭЬЗрЭРµ(рЭСЫвЖСвИТрЭСЫвЖУ). Because most electrons are localised around their respective nuclei, you can
greatly simplify your analysis by defining effective atomic moments, and modelling these as vectors
on a discrete lattice, see fig. 2.1. This also allows you to use the symmetries inherent in crystals, for
example if you can model the magnetic unit cell you can model the whole crystal1.
The atomic moment picture is well-justified in insulators, i.e. systems where a bandgap at the Fermi
level limits electron motion between atoms.
Here you can assign each electron to a given atom,
then use Hunds rules to put electrons into orbitals[6], then add up the electron spins to get a good
approximation of the atomic moment. Figure 2.1 green circle, shows the magnetic/atomic unit cell of
the insulating vdW magnet CrI3. Here, by HundвАЩs rules, the iodine spins cancel, while each cromium
atom has a net moment of 3рЭЬЗрЭРµ.
1A magnetic unit cell in a crystal is a region of space, which when used to tile all of space reproduces the crystal, including
magnetic structure. Clearly, it is equal to or larger than the atomic unit cell.
4
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:11==
In metallic magnets, the absence of a bandgap at the Fermi surface means that electrons can easily
move between atoms and form highly delocalised states. The electrons are then said to be itinerant2.
In itinerant magnets, atomic moments are not simple multiples of the Bohr magneton, рЭЬЗрЭРµ, they may
change during an experiment as electrons reorganise, and parts of the electron sea cannot be ascribed
to any one atom. Nonetheless, you can sometimes meaningfully describe metals in terms of localised
moments, in particular transition metals because the highly localised d- and f-orbitals tend to dominate
the magnetisation[44, chap. 4].
2.2
Models of magnetism
2.2.1
Stoner band theory
Figure 2.3: Stoner model of ferromagnetism. Includes spin-flip excitation.
In the limit of completely itinerant electrons, i.e. the opposite of the local moment picture, one can
use the methods of band theory. The idea, as illustrated in fig. 2.3, is to split the familiar electron
band into and up and a down part. Exchange and correlation effects may raise the energy of one
band relative to another, so that electrons flow to the lower band, i.e. flip their spin. This can be
summarised in an effective exchange-correlation B-field, defined as

==PAGE:12==
In appendix A.1, I use thermodynamic considerations to derive an implicit formula for the critical
temperature in the Stoner model eq. (A.2). This formula was implemented with ab-initio DFT cal-
culations by O. Gunnarson for the 3 elementary ferromagnets, Fe, Co and Ni[23].
Co overshoots
the experimental temperature by a factor of 3, Fe and Ni by around a factor 5. This failure of the
itinerant model suggests that even in these metallic systems, local interactions are crucial for critical
temperature determination.
2.2.2
Equation of state in weakly ferromagnetic metals
A very general approach to studying phase transitions is Landau-Ginsburg theory[20, chap. 9]. The
idea is to expand the free energy, рЭРє, in powers of the ordering parameter, in this case the total
magnetisation, рЭСА. Because the order parameter is small near the critical temperature, low order
terms dominate. Also, odd terms are 0 due to mirror symmetry, so
рЭРєвЙИ1

(2.4)

An extension of this idea was used by Lonzarrich & Tailleffer (L&T) to study weakly ferromagnetic
materials[49].

magnetisation, i.e. the system moment attained if the spin of every polarisable4 electron is aligned.

I observe that, while рЭСАрЭСЙis small near a phase transition, the local magnetisation рЭСЪрЭСР(r) may still
be large. The gradient term gives some contribution from local spin fluctuations, but truncating at
the first derivative is only accurate if the fluctuations vary slowly in space (low q). If you have a
weakly ferromagnetic system, рЭСЪрЭСР(r) is small compared to saturation, рЭСАрЭС†/рЭСЙ, and the comparatively
weak interactions suggests gradual spin variations, which may justify the expansion. However, as
emphasised by Coey et. al. [63], in strong ferromagnets, thermodynamic properties are better captured
in a local-moment picture with localised excitations6.

The origin of exchange coupling is the way spin state indirectly influences the distribution of charge
in space. Through the Pauli exclusion principle, relative spin orientation affects both the degree of
electron confinement and Coulombic repulsion. There are multiple specific mechanisms which can add
up to give рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧeither sign. An interesting example is the RKKY interaction in itinerant magnets, where
coupling between highly localised f-orbitals is mediated by conduction electrons. For more detail, see
[6, 85, 92]. Regardless of the mechanism, exchange is a local interaction. In fact, in insulators it is
often adequate to consider only nearest-neighbour coupling, in which case you can write

A few notes are in order. While the exchange terms induce alignment or anti-alignment, the Dzyaloshinskii-
Moriya Interaction (DMI) induces spiral structures, e.g. skyrmions. The biquadratic term is rarely
included, but a recent study found that it is important in some 2D magnets, including CrI3[35].
Sometimes, certain terms can be excluded a priory by symmetry considerations. For example, imagine
inverting your coordinate system through a point midway between spins S1 and S2. Because spins
are pseudovectors, they are invariant under inversion, except that the positions are exchanged. Hence
D12 вЛЕ(S1 √Ч S2) вЖТрЭРЈ12 вЛЕ(S2 √Ч S1) = вИТD12 вЛЕ(S1 √Ч S2). Thus, if the system is invariant under inversion,
it must hold that D12 = 0. In conclusion; crystals with an inversion center (centrosymmetric) have
zero DMI. In fig. 2.4 for example, if you invert through the midpoint between nearest neighbour
sites (NN) you get the same lattice with blue/orange sites interchanged. Because of this symmetry,
there is no NN-DMI. DMI between next-NN sites is possible though, since inverting through a next-
NN midpoint gives a shifted lattice. In solids, the sort of symmetry breaking that enable DMI and
single-ion anisotropy is generally a result of anisotropy in the crystal lattice. Such anisotropies only
influence the spin degrees of freedom through spin-orbit coupling. As a consequence, certain magnetic
structures and phenomena only occur because of spin-orbit coupling, which is inherently relativistic.

2.2.4
Hybrid models
While the L&T model eq. (2.5) is only applicable to weakly ferromagnetic materials, it has features
that are relevant to all itinerant magnets. The gradient term, while inadequate for localised spin
fluctuations, may capture long-range correlations that are difficult to get with a Heisenberg type
model. Also, including moment magnitude in the Hamiltonian makes this a model variable, which
may capture how spin densities in metals can change dynamically.
Thus, in his highly recommendable textbook вАЭTheory of Itinerant Electron MagnetismвАЭ[44, chap. 5],

2.3
Magnetic excitations
Once you have a model Hamiltonian, determining the classical groundstate is simply a matter of
figuring out which configuration, or degenerate set of configurations, has the lowest energy. Then you
can compute the net magnetisation, or some other relevant ordering parameter, to tell if the system is
magnetic. But at finite temperature, the system may jump to excited states. Thermally excited states
largely determine thermodynamic properties such as heat capacity. It is the low energy excitations
which cause a system with ordered ground state to disorder upon heating, hence the distribution of
low energy excited configurations largely determines critical temperature.
In quantum systems, you talk about the occupation of different modes of excitation. For instance,
in molecular H2, you can count the quanta of vibrational energy in the interatomic bond, i.e. in the
one vibrational mode. Such collective excitations of the electron cloud often behave like particles in
their own right, with quantum statistics and particle-particle interactions. Thus a quantum of lattice
vibration is a phonon. Similarly, the skyrmion in fig. 2.2 is a magnetic quasiparticle, i.e. a localised,
particle-like collective excitation of the electron spin field.
2.3.1
Spin-flips / Stoner excitations
The simplest kind of magnetic excitation is an up/down spin-flip of a single electron, see fig. 2.3.
These so-called Stoner excitations are precisely what you model in the Stoner model of section 2.2.1.
Here, a magnetic groundstate is achieved when Stoner excitations are energetically favoured, and a
phase transition at finite рЭСЗoccurs when thermal excitations induce an excess of spin-flips. As noted
in section 2.2.1, Stoner excitations give far too high an estimate of рЭСЗрЭРґ, which suggests that there are
lower energy modes of excitation with greater importance. In eq. (2.8), a spin-flip costs an energy
of рЭРљрЭСІрЭСЖ2, where рЭСІis the number of nearest-neighbours at each site, i.e. an energy of the order of the
exchange energy.
In a quantum model, you can treat Stoner excitations in terms of the operators9

raise(+)/lower(-) the рЭСІ-component of the spin at site рЭСЦby 1. Because electrons have spin-1/2, this
amounts to a full spin-flip. Let |ќ¶вЯ©be the ferromagnetic groundstate and |рЭСЧвЯ©=

2.3.2
Spin-waves / magnons
Since each electron spin is either up or down, it seems counterintuitive that you can have a lower
energy excitation than a single spin-flip. This is nonetheless the case. Consider a linear combination
of Stoner excitations of the form

Magnon state

where RрЭСЧis the position of the рЭСЧвАЩth lattice site and |рЭСЧвЯ©is given in eq. (2.11). As shown in appendix A.2,

9The operators are typically introduced in introductory quantum mechanics courses in the context of isolated spins, see

Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
9
==PAGE:16==
As seen from eq. (2.12), the magnon is the Fourier transform of the Stoner excitation. Where the
Stoner excitation is a localised spin-flip, the magnon is delocalised; smeared out across all sites, but
both have a spin of 1. The magnon is thus a spin-1 quasiparticle, justifying the suffix -non. As all
integer-spin particles, the magnon obeys Bose-Einstein statistics.
This discussion begs the question; what does a magnon look like? Because the spin-flip is smeared out,
rather than one site flipped, you have a slight reduction in the polar angle, рЭЬГ. The phase factor can
be understood as the spins precessing in the рЭС•рЭС¶-plane from one site to the next, with the azimuthal
angle at site рЭСЦgiven by рЭЬЩрЭСЦ= q вЛЕRрЭСЦ. A magnon may thus be interpreted as the quantum analog of
a spin-wave. Some examples of spin-waves are shown in fig. 2.5. If you have multiple magnons with
the same q, you have get a lower рЭЬГ, at least assuming the magnons do not interact. As you increase
the number of magnons, e.g. by heating, magnon-magnon interactions will yield complicated new
collective excitations that I do not know how to visualise.
In the case of multiple spins per unit cell, as is always the case in antiferromagnets, you have a
sublattice for each unit-cell spin. For each sublattice, you get a separate magnon branch. This is
analogous to phonons in a solid. Formally, it necessitates some extra indices, e.g. рЭРЄ(q) вИТвЖТрЭРЄрЭЬЗ(q), but
the interpretation of magnons is unchanged.
As discussed in section 2.4.3, magnons are central to the thermodynamics of magnets, especially in
low-dimensional systems. Hence much of this thesis concerns the modelling of magnons, in particular
their dispersion relations.
Figure 2.5: Examples of spin-waves in a ferromagnet with different spatial frequency. Left : 1D modes with рЭСЮ=
(рЭЬЛ, рЭЬЛ/3, 2рЭЬЛ/3. Notice that each has a 2рЭЬЛ/рЭСЮperiod. Right : A magnon mode in 2D with q = (рЭСЮрЭС•, рЭСЮрЭС¶) = (рЭЬЛ/2, вИТрЭЬЛ/2).
Notice the period of 4 in both directions and the lines of constant phase running perpendicular to q.
2.3.3
Magnetic susceptibility
You can construct a powerful, general formalism for low energy excitations by working with linear
response functions. One such response function is the magnetic susceptibility рЭЬТрЭСЪ, which relates a
given magnetic field perturbation, рЭЫњB, to a change in magnetisation, рЭЫњm, according to
рЭЫњm(r, рЭС°) = вИЂdrвА≤ вИЂdрЭС°рЭЬТрЭСЪ(r, rвА≤, рЭС°, рЭС°вА≤)рЭЫњB(rвА≤, рЭС°вА≤)
(2.13)
If your system is translation invariant, рЭЬТрЭСЪ(r, rвА≤) = рЭЬТрЭСЪ(r вИТrвА≤) and if time-translation invariant
рЭЬТрЭСЪ(рЭС°, рЭС°вА≤) = рЭЬТрЭСЪ(рЭС°вИТрЭС°вА≤).
Then, the real-space response eq. (2.13) is a convolution, so the Fourier
transform takes the especially simple form
рЭЫњm(q, рЭЬФ) = рЭЬТрЭСЪ(q, рЭЬФ)рЭЫњB(q, рЭЬФ)
(2.14)
10
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:17==
When вДСрЭЬТрЭСЪ(q, рЭЬФ) вЙ†0, energy is transferred from the perturbing field and into the sample.
This
can only happen if there exist one or more quasiparticle modes with frequencies (q, рЭЬФ). Computing
dynamic susceptibility functions thus allows you to map out the whole spectrum of excitation modes.
An example is the analysis of the spin-split free electron gas, fig. 2.6. Here we have 2 parabolic bands,
where one spin, say down, is shifted up in energy by ќФ, fig. 2.6. The spectrum is shown in fig. 2.6.
Stoner excitations occur at all (q, рЭЬФ) values obeying simultaneous momentum and energy conservation,
which may be expressed as вДПрЭЬФ= рЭРЄk+q +ќФвИТрЭРЄq, where k is spin-up momentum[6]. Besides this broad,
continuous Stoner band, there is a single spin-wave mode, i.e. a magnonic dispersion line. When the
(q, рЭЬФ)-value of the spin-wave lies in the Stoner continuum, it will lose energy to Stoner excitations;
a process known as Landau damping. This results in a broadening of the magnon spectrum and a
reduction in quasi-particle lifetime, but does not change the location of the dispersion line.
Figure 2.6: Magnetic excitations in the free electron gas from linear response theory. Modified version of [6, (fig.
7.13a, 7.13d)]. (a) : Spin-split parabolic bands with a Stoner excitation drawn. рЭСБвЖС, рЭСБвЖУare density of states functions.
(b) : A single magnon branch, which is Landau damped when crossing into the continuum of Stoner excitations.
To those with experience in photoelectric excitations, it is interesting to note that the magnon branch
in a free electron gas is entirely analogous to the plasmon branch, the Stoner continuum analogous to
the electron-hole continuum and Landau damping works in the same way.
For a very recent, in-depth introduction to dynamic susceptibility functions in the context of magnetic
spectroscopy, I recommend the work of Skovhus & Olsen [76].
2.4
Critical temperature
The way magnetic order is disrupted is through the proliferation of low energy excitations, as pointed
out in section 2.3. This begs the question : when do you have enough energy in your quasiparticle
modes to say the order is broken? If you have a single magnon quantum at low рЭСЮ, you have a gentle
precession on top of an otherwise ordered state. A bit of noise in the signal, so to speak. The answer is
that ferro/anti-ferromagnetic order constitutes a phase, so alignment-to-disorder constitutes a phase
transition, with an associated critical temperature, рЭСЗрЭРґ.
Below, I present some of the ways to determine рЭСЗрЭРґ.
2.4.1
Susceptibility and Monte Carlo
When balancing on the edge of a phase transition, small changes in magnetic field may push the
spin-lattice over the edge, causing mass alignment of spins, with a resultant, macroscopic change in
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
11
==PAGE:18==
magnetisation. Because of this high sensitivity around the phase transition, рЭЬТрЭСЪ(рЭСЗ) is sharply peaked
at рЭСЗ= рЭСЗрЭРґ.
A high susceptibility also means the system is susceptible to random, thermal fluctuations. This is
encompassed in the relation [20, (eq. 5.17)]
рЭЬТрЭСЪ=
1
рЭСШрЭРµрЭСЗ[рЭСА2 вИТрЭСА

(2.15)
where рЭСАis a classical, ensemble average. The rhs. is the variance in magnetisation per thermal
energy, which measures the extent of magnetic fluctuations. Thus, another sign of an imminent phase
transition is major fluctuations in system properties.
In infinite systems with no defects, рЭЬТрЭСЪdiverges at рЭСЗрЭРґ, so in analytical models, one can identify рЭСЗрЭРґ
by the condition 1/рЭЬТрЭСЪ(рЭСЗрЭРґ) = 0.
In finite systems, the curve is smoothed out, but you still have a very sharp peak, which coincides with
a sharp drop in net magnetisation. This can be used to locate рЭСЗрЭРґin Monte Carlo (MC) simulations.
Here, you essentially sample states from a canonical ensemble at some chosen temperatures, in order
to compute рЭСА(рЭСЗ) and рЭЬТрЭСЪ(рЭСЗ). For more information, see e.g. [34].

An issue with MC algorithms is that computing the energy ab-initio is prohibitively time-consuming.
You need a classical spin-model, for instance eq. (2.8). Even with a phenomenological spin-model,
complicated and long range interactions can make the MC calculation computationally demanding
in its own right. With insulators, the energetics are typically dominated by short range exchange
interactions, рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ. In itinerant magnets however, long-range interactions could be important, resulting
in a lot of non-zero рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ. Also, it is unclear a priory at what interpaticle distance you can neglect рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ,
which is problematic in high-throughput calculations.

This approach entirely neglects local variations in the spin-lattice, thus the transition from order to
disorder is sudden and absolute. In reality, when a spin-lattice is about to disorder, it will tend to split
into domains of different orientation, or to form islands of instability that act as nucleation sites for
further spin-flips. These local effects facilitate a phase transition, thereby lowering рЭСЗрЭРґ, so eq. (2.16)
generally overestimates рЭСЗрЭРґ.
Fluctuations can be incorporated formally at the level of RPA [71]. With a single atom in the unit

The only requirement is a gapless magnon mode, i.e.
one with a 0-energy excitation.
In
any system with full rotational symmetry, uniformly rotating all spins costs 0 energy.
Hence to
avoid gapless modes, it is necessary to have some form of anisotropic interaction to break this рЭСЖрЭСИ(2)
symmetry.
These observations were put on rigorous footing by Mermin and Wagner in 1966[55], building on the
work of Hohenberg[30]. They proved that for a Heisenberg lattice model with рЭСЖрЭСИ(2) symmetry, long-
range ferro- and antiferromagnetic order is impossible at рЭСЗвЙ†0. Recently the results were generalised
to itinerant ferromagnets with no reference to lattices. To quote вАЭHowever, a generalization of the
HMW argument can be used to rule out rigorously the possibility of ferromagnetism at рЭСЗвЙ†0 in any
two-dimensional electron model without spin-orbit coupling or magnetic dipole interactions, which
would destroy SU(2) symmetry in the microscopic Hamiltonian.вАЭ[26].
At a conceptual level, the potential of magnons to preclude order suggests that they are particular
important in low-dimensional systems, even when anisotropic interactions does enable a finite рЭСЗрЭРґ.
This also explains why 2D magnets generally have lower рЭСЗрЭРґthan their bulk counterparts.

2.5
Ab-initio methods
In section 2.4, I showed ways to determine critical temperature from magnetic excitations, in particular
magnon dispersion relations. Here, I present ways to determine the relevant excitation spectra of real
materials ab-initio, i.e. with only unambiguous, material parameters as input. This typically means
the positions and atomic constituents of the crystal lattice in question.
In order to be computationally tractable, ab-initio simulation methods come with their own set of
simplifying assumptions and limitations, on top of those in the possible spin-models. A summary of
the chosen computational scheme, ordered into layers of approximation is seen in fig. 2.8. Note that
all of outlined methods are strictly applicable at рЭСЗ= 0. Finite temperature effects are generally not
treated by pure ab-initio, due to the computational difficulty of simulating an ensemble of different
states.
2.5.1
The electronic structure problem
The most fundamental, widely accepted model of reality that physicists have come up with is the
standard model of particle physics. However, the structure and low energy excitations of materials are
largely determined by the electromagnetic interactions of electrons and protons. Neglecting irrelevant
particles, we are left with the Dirac equation, which is a quantum model of spin-1/2 particles which
is consistent with the theory of special relativity.
Because material physics is generally in a low temperature regime, relativistic effects tend to play a
minor role. Therefore, the starting point of most electronic structure theory is the non-relativistic
Schr√ґedinger equation
ћВ
рЭРїSchr√ґedinger |рЭЬУвЯ©= рЭРЄ|рЭЬУвЯ©
where
ћВ
рЭРїSchr√ґedinger =
ћВрЭСЗion +
ћВрЭСИion-ion +
ћВрЭСЗe +
ћВрЭСИe-ion +
ћВрЭСИee
(2.20)
Here
ћВрЭСЗion,
ћВрЭСЗe are kinetic energy operators of the nuclei (which are positive ions) and electrons respec-
tively,
ћВрЭСИion-ion,
ћВрЭСИion-e,
ћВрЭСИe-e are operators for Coulombic interaction.
Neglecting relativistic effects entirely is not generally well-justified, especially in the context of mag-
netism. As mentioned in section 2.2.3, spin-orbit coupling is inherently relativistic and enable precisely
the sort of anisotropic interactions that are necessary for long-range, magnetic order in low-dimensional
systems, see section 2.4.3. Starting from the Dirac equation, you can derive a spin-orbit coupling term
and other relativistic corrections to your Hamiltonian, or treat such effects formally through pertur-
bation theory, see e.g. [44]. This is beyond the scope of the present thesis.
Magnetic structure is a feature of the electron cloud, so nuclei only matter to the extend they influence
the electrons. A nucleus is about 103 to 105 times as massive as an electron depending on element,
hence so much slower that the nuclei are essentially stationary from the electron perspective. Conse-
quently, I use the Born-Oppenheimer approximation of treating the nuclei as stationary. This does
remove things like phonon-magnon coupling, but nothing relevant to this thesis.
The above simplifications lead to the electronic structure Hamiltonian
ћВ
рЭРїe-structure =
ћВрЭСЗe +
ћВрЭСИe-ion +
ћВрЭСИee +
ћВ
рЭРїrelativistic corrections
(2.21)
2.5.2
DFT in a very small nutshell
Density functional theory is a popular way to do ab-initio electronic structure calculations, which
replaces eq. (2.21) with the closely related Kohn-Sham Hamiltonian,
ћВ

This deals with 2 technical issues of eq. (2.21).
Firstly, the wavefunction of an рЭСБelectron system lives in a 3N dimensional space (one for each position
coordinate of each electron).
The number of parameters required to model such a wavefunction

Secondly, exchange and correlation effects makes it exceedingly difficult to solve eq. (2.21), especially

The Kohn-Sham solution is to throw all the exchange and correlation effects together in an
approximate, single particle potential called
ћВрЭС£рЭС•рЭСР, then approximate

ћВрЭС£рЭС•рЭСР, is the central, simplifying assumption in DFT. Consequently, there are hundreds
of different, more or less case specific xc-potentials in use[51].
These modifications of eq. (2.20) are justified by the 2 Hohenberg-Kohn theorems[29]. The first asserts
that there is a one-to-one mapping between groundstate electron density, рЭСЫрЭСФрЭС†, and Hamiltonian. рЭСЫрЭСФрЭС†
thus implicitly determines the groundstate wavefunction, so рЭСЫрЭСФрЭС†contains the same information as said
wavefunction. The second theorem asserts that minimising рЭРЄрЭС°рЭСЬрЭС°[рЭСЫ] does yield the true groundstate
and groundstate energy.
The way to minimise рЭРЄрЭС°рЭСЬрЭС°in practice is outlined in fig. 2.9.

for non-interacting electrons. All other contributions are described with an effective single-particle potential, рЭС§рЭСТрЭСУрЭСУ,
which includes exchange and correlation effects.
The original Hohenberg-Kohn-Sham formalism did not deal with spin, but was later generalised to
spin-polarised systems[4], including the Hohenberg-Kohn theorems[64]. Here the fundamental variable
is the 2-by-2 spinor density ћВрЭСЫ(r), see eq. (4.2). I present more of the formalism and algebraic expressions
in chapter 4. For more background, there are textbooks on DFT in general, e.g.[39], and at least one
specifically in the context of itinerant magnetism[45].
2.5.3
The issue of local correlations
Because DFT treats correlation effects approximately, it is arguably unsuitable for systems with
strongly correlated electrons. An example of materials with local correlations is transition metals, like
iron and niobium, because of interactions between localised d- and f-orbitals. These same localised
orbitals are central to magnetism.
Local correlations can be added within the framework of DFT by extra terms in the xc-functional,
рЭРЄрЭС•рЭСР, e.g. with the popular Hubbard U-functional.[27] Alternatively, you can treat electron-electron
interactions perturbatively in the so-called GW approximations[38], however at the price of much
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
17
==PAGE:24==
heavier computations. Entirely local correlations can be treated non-perturbatively using Dynam-
ical Mean-Field Theory (DMFT), where the electronic structure problem is mapped on to a local
many-body problem that can be solved numerically[42]. DMFT in its basic form only takes local cor-
relations into account, neglecting nonlocal ones like intersite exchange[1]. There exist various versions
of DMFT, and proposed improvements, e.g. DMFT+DFT schemes, but all are highly demanding
computationally[38].
2.5.4
TDDFT : an ab-initio response theory
DFT is inherently a static theory. You solve a time-independent Schr√ґedinger equation, possibly in
the presence of static external potentials. Therefore DFT does not directly allow the calculation of
dynamic effects, like the energy dispersions of excitations. One option is to use the extended formalism
of Time-Dependent Density Functional Theory (TDDFT). The idea of TDDFT is to set up a time
dependent problem with perturbations from dynamic external fields. One may then derive ab-initio
expressions for the relevant linear response functions.
For example, in the case of a scalar, electric potential вИТрЭСТрЭЫњрЭЬЩрЭСТрЭС•рЭС°, the dynamic density-response function
for a set of non-interacting electrons, written in real-space, is11[9]

The method in [77] is already implemented in GPAW, but due to the great number of (q, рЭЬФ)-points
necessary to resolve a magnon spectrum, the calculation is vastly more demanding than groundstate
DFT. This limits the size and complexity of systems that can be studied, and is a hindrance to
high-throughput studies.
In the succeeding chapters, I derive and demonstrate a method for computing magnon spectra which
only requires the рЭЬФвИТвЖТ0, static limit of a non-interacting response function. This greatly reduces the
number and complexity of response calculations, making the algorithm faster, hence better suited for
high-throughput studies. The cost is the use of spin-model Hamiltonians, see section 2.2.3, to bridge
the gap between DFT and dynamic, finite temperature physics.

3
Magnon spectrum from Heisenberg model
In section 2.4 I showed how the critical temperature for magnetic order, рЭСЗрЭРґ, can be derived from the
magnon dispersion relation, рЭРЄрЭЬЗ(q). In this chapter, I use a fairly general quantum spin-lattice model,
see section 2.2.3, to derive an eigenvalue problem for the spectrum of non-interacting magnons. Then
I show how one can modify this expression by treating magnon-magnon interactions at a mean-field
level; the so-called Random Phase Approximation (RPA)1.
3.1
Multi-sublattice notation
Figure 3.1: Sketch of generic lattice to illustrate notation. RрЭСЫis a reference point in the рЭСЫвАЩth magnetic unit cell, рЭЬПрЭЬИ

to the origin, r is position relative to the origin, and rрЭСЫрЭЬИ= r вИТRрЭСЫвИТрЭЬПрЭЬИis the position relative to the рЭЬИвАЩth site in
the рЭСЫвАЩth cell. Throughout the thesis, I denote рЭЬИ, рЭЬЗas sublattice indices, рЭСЫ, рЭСЪas cell indices and рЭСЦ= рЭЬЗрЭСЫ, рЭСЧ= рЭЬИрЭСЪas
site indices.

The model includes isotropic exchange, рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ, anisotropic exchange,

3.3
Holstein-Primakoff bosonisation
3.3.1
Definition
The Holstein-Primakoff (HP) bosonisation scheme is a way to map spin operators onto bosonic rais-
ing/lowering operators. It is defined by
ћВрЭСЖрЭСІ
рЭЬЗрЭСЫ= рЭСЖрЭЬЗвИТћВрЭСЫрЭЬЗрЭСЫ

1

ћВрЭСЫрЭЬЗрЭСЫ=

I note that for the vacuum of the HP operators, it holds that
ћВрЭСЫрЭЬЗрЭСЫ|0вЯ©рЭЬЗрЭСЫ= 0 =вЗТ
ћВрЭСЖрЭСІ
рЭЬЗрЭСЫ|0вЯ©рЭЬЗрЭСЫ= рЭСЖрЭЬЗ|0вЯ©рЭЬЗрЭСЫ
which precisely describes the ferromagnetic groundstate. The HP operators are thus raising/lowering
operators for spin-flip excitations in the ferromagnetic groundstate. As discussed in section 2.3.2, the
Fourier transform of a spin-flip excitation is a quantum of a spin-wave, i.e. a magnon.
3.3.2
How to use the HP-formalism
The usefulness of the HP-formalism largely stems from how you can Taylor expand the вИЪ... part to
systematically deal with different orders of magnon interaction. The interaction order, i.e. the number
of magnons colliding in the interaction, is given by the number of HP-operators in the relevant term.
It is not clear a priori that this expansion is justified. Indeed, even at рЭСЗ= 0, vacuum occupation of the
magnon modes may disrupt the magnetic order, leading to a proliferation of quasiparticle excitations.
However, it stands to a material with a stable, ordered ground state, the the number of magnons at
low рЭСЗis relatively small. With few magnons, multi-particle collisions are rare, so higher order terms
will be of lesser importance. I note that the number of HP-operators in every term scales with the
exponent of рЭСЖрЭЬЗ, according to рЭСЖрЭС•
рЭЬЗћВрЭСО2(1вИТрЭС•)
рЭЬЗрЭСЫ
. Higher order terms thus decay faster the higher the total spin
at each site is.
In any case, when expanding
ћВ
рЭРїin powers of
ћВрЭСЫрЭЬЗрЭСЫ
рЭСЖрЭЬЗ, you get
ћВ
рЭРї= рЭРЄ0
вПЯ
рЭТ™(рЭСЖ2)
+
ћВ
рЭРї1/2
вПЯ

It is a general rule that
ћВ

ћВ

of non-interacting magnons can be read off.
ћВ

a model of non-interacting magnons, renormalised by pair interactions treated at the mean-field level.
This approach allows you to derive matrix eigenvalue problems for the bare- and renormalised magnon
spectra.

3.5

The eigenvalue equation eq. (3.17) only applies to non-interacting magnons. To incorporate interac-

4
Exchange coupling from perturbation
theory
4.1
Obtaining Heisenberg parameters ab initio
To recap; once you have the parameters for the Heisenberg model eq. (2.6), the magnon spectrum can
be computed as the eigenvalue spectrum of the matrix eq. (3.17). With this in hand, you can calculate
the critical, ordering temperature, рЭСЗрЭРґ, using any of the methods in section 2.4.
This begs the question; how does one get spin-model parameters from ab initio simulations?
In
other words, how to connect a somewhat arbitrary spin-model, рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСА, to the DFT Hamiltonian of
a specific material,
ћВ
рЭРїрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ? The simplest method is to calculate the total energy of different spin
configurations with
ћВ
рЭРїрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ, then fit the result with рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСА. One option is the supercell method, where
compute the energy of different collinear configurations across multiple unit cells[89, 93]. Alternatively,
you can get Fourier components, рЭРљ(q), directly by simulating spin-spiral states[68], however this
entails the technical difficulty of simulating non-collinear spin structures with constrained moments[88].
Another option is to model the рЭСЗ> рЭСЗрЭРґparamagnetic phase, through Disordered Local-Moment (DLM)
picture[61, 79]. Here one simulates randomly generated spin configurations with fixed moments[44,
section 5.5].
Rather than comparing energy changes by computing multiple moment configurations, one may com-
pare the energy changes in рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСАand
ћВ
рЭРїрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗformally, by pen-and-paper algebra. Because the same
exchange parameters, рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ, are supposed to model rotations whether the rotated angle is рЭЬЛor рЭЬЛ/1000,
one can advantageously compare infinitesimal rotations, which unlocks all the machinery of pertur-
bation theory. This is precisely the philosophy of the magnetic force theorem, originally applied by
Liechtenstein, Katsnelson, Antropov and Gubanov (LKAG) in 1987[48], to derive a formula for рЭРљрЭСЦрЭСЧ
in terms of so-called scattering matrices. The method has been extensively tested since[91, 58, 87,
81, 86]. For a comparison LKAG with supercell methods, see e.g. the work of Bl√Љgel et. al. [46,
95]. The issue is that the formalism of (L&K) only works with the KKR formalism of DFT1, which is
incompatible with the PAW2 formalism used in GPAW.
The LKAG formula has since been extended to non-collinear configurations[3], to the LCAO3 formal-
ism of DFT[53, 54], to Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya interactions[69] and to resolve the exchange interaction
of individual orbitals within an atom[78, 36]. For itinerant magnets, the importance of delocalised
states and long-range interactions makes it sensible to use a plane-wave basis for the DFT calcula-
tions, which is one of GPAWs three core basis sets. The LKAG formula has technically been extended
to plane-wave calculations[41], however only where the plane-waves are used to construct a basis of
Wannier functions. Wannier functions are similar to localised, atomic orbitals, except defined for
periodic systems[52]. Using a Wannier function basis greatly limits the freedom to vary simulations
parameters, the ability to do systematic convergence studies and you need to associate the magnetic
moments directly with specific Wannier functions.
Thus, despite more than three decades of development since the original LKAG paper[48], there exist
no pure plane-wave version. Therefore, I did the only reasonable thing and derived my own plane-
1Korringa-Kohn-Rostocker formalism. Here you compute the electronic band structure by solving a multiple scattering
problem[44, section 3]. Well-suited for impurities, local defects and the DLM picture.
2Projector-Augmented Wave method [5].
3Linear Combination of Atomic Orbitals.
It refers to the use of atomic orbitals as a basis set for real-space DFT
calculations. Well-suited to spatially localised states.
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
25
==PAGE:32==
wave method. The LKAG paper explicitly uses the KKR scattering theory, and almost all later papers
hardcode the notion of molecular orbitals and other discrete sets of localised states into their formal
derivations[78, 58, 53, 54]. Also, in some cases I am not convinced of the mathematical rigour[53, 54].
The only derivation I have found, which does not presuppose a specific DFT formalism or basis set, is
an extremely concise 4 page letter by Patrick Bruno[7]. The point of the letter is actually an argument
that the LKAG formula should be вАЭrenormalisedвАЭ to include the effect of a constraining рЭРµ-field, so
he goes into minimal detail regarding how he rederived the bare LKAG formula. In this chapter, I
present my own reverse engineered, substantially elaborated version of Brunos derivation. Because I
did not use the renormalised formula, I omit the constraining field entirely in this presentation.
4.2
Formalism and definitions
4.2.1
Problem statement
The goal is to derive a useful expression for рЭРљRRвА≤ in terms of quantities that can be computed ab
initio. Specifically, I use DFT with the LDA-functional as my xc-functional.
The derivation goes as follows : (1) Write down a classical spin-model, рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСА. (2) Write down a
DFT energy functional рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ. (3) Compute the energy change with рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСАfor a small rotation applied
to the classical, ferromagnetic ground state. (4) Apply an equivalent, small rotation to the spinor
density operator,
ћВрЭСЫ.
(5) Compute the energy change in рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗto second order in rotation angle
self-consistently. (6) Compare the 2 energy variations to get a useful equality.
4.2.2
Spin-model
I consider the isotropic Heisenberg model, eq. (2.6), but with the notation
рЭРїрЭСЖрЭСА({uR}) = вИТ1

coupling parameters to be determined from DFT calculations. By symmetry рЭРљRRвА≤ = рЭРљRвА≤R. Note that
the moment magnitudes are incorporated into рЭРљRRвА≤.

4.3.3
Intermezzo : Brunos constraining field renormalisation
Because
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСЬрЭС°,
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩare constructed by fixing rotation angles, so that only some quantities may seek
energetic minima, neither
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСЬрЭС°nor
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩare physically realisable, steady states. This is where Bruno
argues that one should use the formalism of constrained DFT[12], and impose a constraining рЭРµ-
field, in order to make
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩa self-consistent ground state. At a formal level, the constraining field
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
29
==PAGE:36==
contributes to linear order in energy, leading to a renormalisation of the exchange parameters. Bruno
and collaborators[86] never implemented the renormalised formulas though. They only tested a formal
modification of the formula eq. (2.17) by computing рЭСЗрЭРґfor Fe, Co and Ni. Liechtenstein & Katsnelson
pointed out that, while Brunos renormalisation should improve temperature estimates, it gives worse
magnon spectra[37]. In any case, I neglected the constraining field renormalisation in my numerical
work, so for clarity, I also neglect it in this derivation.
4.4
Self-consistent perturbation theory
Note that I use the eigenstates and eigenvalues of the unperturbed system below, i.e.
the ones
corresponding to
ћВ
рЭРїрЭРЊрЭСЖ[ ћВрЭСЫ0].
I denote these рЭЬУрЭСЦрЭЫЉand рЭЬЦрЭСЦрЭЫЉ, rather than e.g.
рЭЬЦ0
рЭСЦрЭЫЉ, to avoid notational
clutter.
4.4.1
Energy variation : what to neglect
We are interested in the energy change associated with infinitesimal spin rotations, which may be
written
ќФрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ= рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ[ ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩ] вИТрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ[ ћВрЭСЫ0]
(4.18)
This is exact within LDA, but only if we use
ћВрЭСЫвЛЖi.e. include relaxation of рЭСЫ, рЭСЪafter rotation by рЭЫњu.
This relaxation is quantified by рЭЫњрЭСЫ= рЭСЫвИЧвИТрЭСЫ0, рЭЫњрЭСЪ= рЭСЪвИЧвИТрЭСЪ0. There are a number of arguments
suggesting that the relaxation is a higher order effect, hence negligible.
First off, the magnetic force theorem, presented in the appendix of [48], gives that to first order in
рЭЫњрЭСЫ, рЭЫњрЭСЪ, the variation of рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗis
рЭЫњрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ= рЭЫњвИЧрЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ= рЭЫњвИЧ(вИС
рЭСЦрЭЫЉ
рЭЬЦрЭСЦрЭЫЉ)
where рЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГis the sum of single-particle Kohn-Sham energies and рЭЫњвИЧis a variation with рЭСЫ, рЭСЪheld fixed.
Note though, that this result is derived specifically for LDA. Written differently, this means that
ќФрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ= рЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ[ ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСЬрЭС°] вИТрЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ[ ћВрЭСЫ0] + рЭТ™2(рЭЫњрЭСЫ, рЭЫњрЭСЪ)
(4.19)
where рЭТ™рЭСЭ(рЭС•, рЭС¶, ...) is a quantity of order рЭСЭor higher in рЭС•, рЭС¶, ...
I rederived this result from scratch in appendix D.3.
The second argument is one of symmetry. Since рЭЫњрЭСЫ(r), рЭЫњрЭСЪ(r) are induced by the rotation, they must
scale with the rotated polar angle рЭЬГ(r). Suppressing r dependence
рЭЫњрЭСЫ= рЭСОрЭЬГ+ рЭСПрЭЬГ2 + рЭТ™(рЭЬГ3)
and likewise for рЭЫњрЭСЪ. But by symmetry, you will get the exact same result from a rotation of вИТрЭЬГ, so
рЭСОрЭЬГ+ рЭСПрЭЬГ2 + рЭСРрЭЬГ3 + ... = вИТрЭСОрЭЬГ+ рЭСПрЭЬГ2 вИТрЭСРрЭЬГ3 + ...
Because this is true for all рЭЬГ, the equality holds coefficient by coefficient, so рЭСО= рЭСР= ... = 0. It means
that рЭЫњрЭСЫ, рЭЫњрЭСЪвИЉрЭТ™(рЭЬГ2). Inserting in eq. (4.19), we see that
ќФрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ= рЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ[ ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСЬрЭС°] вИТрЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ[ ћВрЭСЫ0] + рЭТ™(рЭЬГ4)
(4.20)
In conclusion, to get the lowest order energy change wrt. a rotation, it is sufficient to consider the
exact rotation while keeping the ground state values of рЭСЫ, рЭСЪfixed, which is precisely what is done in
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСЬрЭС°[eq. (4.17)].

4.4.2
Hamiltonian perturbation
The idea is to determine the variation of

4.5
Exchange coupling formula
Comparing eq. (4.25) with eq. (4.16), I conclude that
рЭРљ(r, rвА≤) = 2рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР(r)рЭЬТ+вИТ
рЭРЊрЭСЖ(r, rвА≤)рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР(rвА≤)
(4.26)
This is it; a rigorously derived formula for the Heisenberg exchange parameters, in terms of ab initio
computable quantities. Because there is no reference to any lattices, basis functions or specific DFT
formalism, it is a highly versatile starting point from which to apply further approximations, e.g. the
discretisation necessary for numerical work.
4.5.1
Assumptions
Here are the main assumptions leading to eq. (4.26)
вАҐ Ferromagnetism : I am not sure if a continuous exchange function, рЭРљ(r, rвА≤), in the Heisenberg
model makes sense for an antiferromagnet.
вАҐ Collinearity : A non-collinear system would be considerably harder to deal with formally.
вАҐ LDA : The LDA approximation of exchange/correlation effects is used explicitly in appendix D.3.
There is no conceptual reason the results would not generalise to other рЭС•рЭСР-functionals; indeed
Solovyev considers a broader class than the LDA in his work [78]. However, the whole approach
is still constrained by the standard approximations of DFT.
вАҐ Perturbation theory is valid : Assuming the spin-model eq. (4.1) holds for all magnetic
configurations, i.e. for any u(r), the exchange parameters are given byeq. (4.26). However, for a
real material, the optimal exchange parameters, and the applicability of the simple Heisenberg
model, may vary between configurations. In this case, it is not guaranteed, even in theory, that
energy fitting methods and perturbation theory formulas should give the same result.
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
33
==PAGE:40==
5
Exchange coupling in plane wave basis
In chapter 4 I derived a general, DFT compatible expression for real-space Heisenberg exchange
parameters eq. (4.26). Here I modify and rewrite the expression further, to a form suitable for a DFT
implementation in a pure plane-wave basis. Besides the novelty of the method, a plane-wave basis
is especially suitable for metals, as it allows for efficient computation of delocalised states. Also, the
exchange parameters are computed in Fourier space, which is the most direct way to get magnon
spectra.
5.1
Discretisation
Ultimately, we need a lattice model in order to solve the magnon eigenvalue problem eq. (3.17). The
magnetic moments in transition-metals are mostly made of the spin of electrons in d- and f-orbitals,
which are fairly localised on the magnetic atoms. Therefore, I choose to associate the spin-model
moments with the integrated spin-densities in particular regions of space;
MR = вИЂ
ќ©R
m(r)dr
(5.1)
Here R is the position of a magnetic site in the spin-model, ќ©R is an integration volume associated
with site R and m(r) = TrрЭСЖ{ ћВрЭСЫ(r) ћВрЭЬОis the spin density from eq. (4.2).
While it is often natural to equate R with the positions of magnetic atoms, as in fig. 6.2, I emphasise
that this is not necessary, nor always optimal. In a material with 2 closely spaced magnetic atoms,
one could use both as a single site. Also, if you e.g. have a strongly magnetic atom sitting among
weakly magnetic ones, you might use a very large integration volume to include some of the weakly
magnetic environment.
In any case, eq. (4.26) is straightforwardly discretised by
рЭРљRRвА≤ = вИЂ

6
Numerical Implementation
In this chapter, I explain my numerical implementation of the exchange coupling formula, eq. (5.9),
which I derived in the preceding two chapters. Because the implementation is novel, I present a detailed
convergence study, both to demonstrate numerical stability and benchmark precision. Due to the
large number of simulations necessary for such benchmarking, I primarily considered the elementary
ferromagnets; iron, cobalt and nickel (Fe, Co, Ni).
6.1
Computational scheme
The procedure for determining
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИis shown in fig. 6.1. First I select a material and a set of q-
points at which to evaluate рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q). Then I perform a standard spin-polarised, self-consistent DFT
calculation for the electronic ground state, as sketched in fig. 2.9. Then I compute рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР
G once, and the
remaining quantities once per q-point. The calculation of рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР
G and рЭЬТ+вИТ
рЭРЊрЭСЖ,GGвА≤(q) is done with the code
developed by Thorbj√Єrn Skovhus for doing TDDFT response theory, so I refer to his article for details
of implementation [77]. The site-kernel calculation is described below, section 6.2.
This really yields
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q), but as discussed in chapter 5, this is adequate for getting magnon dispersion
relations, рЭРЄрЭЬЗ(q).
For computing рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q), I uniformly sample the first Brillouin zone to compute
1
рЭСБвИСq
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q), then subtract this average as in eq. (5.11).
I note that the only computationally demanding steps are the DFT ground state calculation, computing
рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР
q
and repeatedly computing рЭЬТ+вИТ
рЭРЊрЭСЖ,GGвА≤. The latter is usually the most time consuming part when
you have more than around 10 q-points. Comparing with fig. 2.10 I note that my algorithm only
requires the non-interacting response function, and only for q-points, not (q, рЭЬФ), so you have a faster
calculation done far fewer times. Memorywise, having to store the full ground state solution during
the heavy matrix algebra of the TDDFT cycle can be a limiting factor in terms of system size.

==PAGE:44==
6.2
Site kernels
Figure 6.2: Sketch of magnetic system with integration regions. My calculations have all used spherical integration
volumes centered on magnetic atoms, but I emphasise that the implementation does not require them to be spherical
or atom centered.
A major part of the algorithm is the selection of site kernels, as this is how the magnetic lattice is
chosen. It is possible to choose an arbitrary shape, then perform the integral in eq. (5.10) numerically,
It is, however, simpler and more numerically stable to choose a shape where the integral can be solved
analytically, e.g. spheres or ellipsis. Choosing such high-symmetry shapes also limits the number of
shape parameters, making it easy to compare simulations systematically. In this thesis, I only tested
spherical regions, centered on the magnetic atoms.

6.3
DFT implementation
I implemented the algorithm of fig. 6.1 in the Python based, open source electronic structure software
GPAW1[57, 14], which uses the projector-augmented wave method[5]. I simulated a plane-wave basis
1Link to website https://wiki.fysik.dtu.dk/gpaw/
38
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:45==
on an (рЭСБрЭСШ, рЭСБрЭСШ, рЭСБрЭСШ) Monkhorst-Pack grid in reciprocal space (рЭСШ-space).
I only used the LDA-xc-
functional2. For the response calculations, the ALDA is used[77], which describes the response of a
homogeneous electron gas to slowly varying external fiels3. The system is considered converged when,
over 3 iterations, the energy change is less than 10вИТ8eV per valence electron and the maximum force on
any atom is 10вИТ8eV/√Е. Reaching this condition ends the self-consistent cycle. Because the simulations
are run at рЭСЗ= 0, as almost all DFT simulations are, there should be a sharp drop from full occupancy
of states below the fermi level, to zero occupancy above. Such a sharp transition sometimes makes
convergence difficult, so an artificial Fermi-Dirac distribution at рЭСЗ= 0.01eV is used.
6.3.1
Sampling рЭСШ-space
Figure 6.3: Brillouin zone of a Face-Centered Cubic (fcc) crystal with high-symmetry lines and high-symmetry points
drawn. Taken from wikipedia https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brillouin_zone
An example of the Brillouin zone for a bulk crystal is seen in fig. 6.3. The DFT simulation simulation
grid and the q-points where рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИis evaluated are both to be found within such a Brillouin zone. In
fact, the TDDFT algorithm can only compute q vectors that match differences between k-points4.
Thus, the simulation grid also sets the k-space resolution of рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q) and by extension of the predicted
magnon spectra.
It is often useful to simulate high-symmetry points, especially when comparing
different simulations. To optimise the number of high-symmetry points matching the k-grid, most of
my simulations use a multiple of 6 as k-space sampling (рЭСБрЭСШ). When simulating band spectra, it is
customary to simulate contiguous paths, going by straight lines between high-symmetry points, e.g.
ќУ вИТвЖТрЭСЛвИТвЖТрЭРЊвИТвЖТќУ. For computing рЭСЗрЭРґ, I use a uniform sampling of k-space.
6.3.2
Simulation parameters
The parameters that are varied from simulation to simulation and need to be converged are shown
below. The names in parentheses are short hands used in my code and plots
вАҐ Plane-wave energy cutoff (pw): The maximum energy of the plane-wave modes used in the
basis set. Typical values are on the order of 100 вИТ1000eV. Units : eV.
2The LDA is full ab initio (no fitting parameters), relatively simple, and often works well in metals. This is because the
high electron mobility gives a relatively smooth electron distribution, which is good when modelling the density function as
locally flat.
3Because I am only using the static limit of the Kohn-Sham response function, the assumption of slowly varying fields is
extremely well-justified.
4For example, if your grid includes the k-points k1 = (0.5, 1.5, 2.5) and k2 = (вИТ0.5, вИТ0.5, вИТ0.5) you can compute
q = (1, 2, 3).
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
39
==PAGE:46==
вАҐ k-space sampling (kpts): How many points used in each dimension of the simulation grid.
Besides being crucial for the accuracy of the ground state calculation, (kpts) is the absolute
number of points in each direction. Another option would have been k-point density, i.e. points
per √Е
вИТ1 of the Brillouin zone. Working with absolute numbers makes it easier to ensure the
k-point grid matches the high-symmetry points of the crystal under study. Units : √Е
вИТ1.
вАҐ Number of bands to converge (nbands): How many electronic energy bands need to be
converged; occupied and otherwise. I always converge all occupied bands and all bands in the
outermost shell that has at least some bands occupied, and a certain number of unoccupied
bands on top of that. Units : Number.
вАҐ Vacuum layer : In a bulk solid, GPAW uses periodic boundary conditions in all directions.
For 2D materials, you still have periodic boundary conditions, so to avoid spurious interactions
with copies of the system along the рЭСІ-axis, I introduce a vacuum layer. The plane-wave basis
extends into this vacuum layer, so the thicker the layer, the heavier the simulations. Especially
with a plane-wave basis because the basis stretch far away from the atoms. Units : √Е
вАҐ Broadening parameter (eta) : The рЭЬВin eq. (2.22). This describes broadening of spectral
lines. It should be 0, but a tiny, finite value improves numerical stability. Units : eV
вАҐ G-vector energy cutoff (ecut) : Like the plane-waves, all G-vectors are simulated up to
some maximum cutoff in energy. |G| is how far you are from the k-space origin and the number
of G-vectors scales as |G|
рЭРЈ, i.e. as volume in 3D systems. As a rule-of-thumb, which is exact
for parabolic bands, рЭРЄвИЉрЭСШ2, so
рЭРЇрЭСЪрЭСОрЭС•вИЉвИЪрЭРЄрЭСРрЭСҐрЭС°рЭСЬрЭСУрЭСУ=вЗТNumber of G-vectors вИЉрЭРЄрЭРЈ/2
рЭСРрЭСҐрЭС°рЭСЬрЭСУрЭСУ
Same argument applies to the plane-wave basis. Units : eV
вАҐ Radius of integration spheres (rc) : Whatever shape parameters are needed for your in-
tegration regions, it is a good idea to test them. When using spheres, the only parameter is
radius. Units : √Е.
Note that the last 3 parameters do not influence the ground state calculation.
6.4
Convergence study
I benchmarked my algorithm by computing magnon energy, рЭРЄ(q), and exchange parameter, рЭРљ(q), at
selected points along high-symmetry lines in the Brillouin zones of bulk Fe(bcc), Co(fcc) and Ni(fcc).
For fcc crystals, I used the ќУ вИТвЖТрЭСЛpath, while for bcc5, I used ќУ вИТвЖТрЭРї. The k-grids were chosen so
that the same q-points were included in all simulations.
The most difficult to converge parameters are ecut, fig. 6.4, and kpts, fig. 6.5. For ecut, the we see
a steady rise after around 300eV, which flattens out gradually. Adding G-vectors adds high frequency
components, so while their might be some destructive interference, it seems reasonable the energy
would tend to increase overall. For kpts, the fluctuations look periodic, with a decaying envelope.
Probably, this is because one can be more or less lucky in terms of which k-points are included at a
given sampling rate, but the consistent decrease in uncertainty is promising.
The remaining parameters are shown in fig. F.1, i.e. eta, pw and nbands. All are rather similar,
with easily achieved convergence. The effect of rc is discussed in chapter 7.

6.4.1
Benchmarking results
Based on the convergence study, I conclude that eta can be set low enough to have no impact.
Increasing pw, mostly slows the ground state calculation, which is not the most time consuming part.
can be set high enough to cause, so pw can be set conservatively enough to not be an issue. nbands
also converge quite easily, which is fortunate, since it is important for computation time. The two
trickiest parameters are kpts and ecut, i.e. k-space sampling and G-vector cutoff. For larger systems,
these will have to be adjusted according to the required level of accuracy. In table 6.1, I show some
values that seem appropriate for ensuring the simulation falls within the 3 confidence bands of 2%,

7
Results and discussion
7.1
Elementary ferromagnetic metals : Fe, Co, Ni
In fig. 7.1, I compare my simulations of Fe, Co, Ni with those of Halilov et. al.[25]. Halilov et. al.
does use the same spin-model and LDA-functiona, but extract their exchange parameters by fitting
to total-energy calculations of spin-spiral states. Central to their theory is the adiabatic assumption,
which states that spin rotations occur across a much longer time-scale than electron motion, including
spin-flip excitations, so the spin-wave dynamics can be treated adiabatically. This is justified by noting
the relatively low energy scale of magnons (fractions of an eV), which is much lower than the Fermi
energy of conduction electrons (вИЉ5eV). The characteristic frequency of spin-waves is thus similarly
orders of magnitude below that of spin-flip dynamics. This fits well with my use of a non-interacting,
static response-function. Indeed, the agreement between my method and Halilov et. al.вАЩs results are
excellent.
In fig. 7.2, I look at the influence of рЭСЯрЭСРfor the different metals, i.e. the radius of the one integration
sphere. The presence of this plateau strongly suggests that there is some region where all the important
spin-density is contained. Then, as the integration spheres start overlapping, the formalism breaks.
Nonetheless, the plateau strongly suggests that the notion of a spatially, localised magnetic moment
is justified despite the itinerant character of the system. This is an important validation of the whole
method.
In fig. 7.3 I show two spectrum calculations for other crystal structures of the elementary ferromagnets.
The negative energy values of spin-waves in Fe(fcc) suggests that the ferromagnetic state is not the
true ground state, which is indeed the case.
This is particularly interesting, as it shows how my
calculational scheme may be used for determining the kind of magnetic order in a system, and test the
stability of a proposed ordering. In Co(hcp), it is interesting to note the presence of 2 magnon bands,
which is to be expected, given the 2 atoms in the unit cell. Also, note how changing рЭСЯрЭСРacross a wide
range of values mostly influences the energy scaling, with modest impact on the overall lineshape.
This again suggests numerical stability, and that the notion of localised states for modelling these
itinerant magnets is justified.

spectrum appears reasonable, with no kinks or random fluctuations to suggest lack of convergence.
Again there is a strong indication of localisation, though not as clear as Fe, Co, Ni. Further study

perhaps in the ligand bonds between chromium and iodine.
7.3
Critical Temperatures
In table 7.1, I present computed critical temperatures for Fe, Co, Ni using the procedure in the table
text. The temperatures are all too low, even рЭСЗрЭСАрЭРєрЭРі
рЭРґ
which is guaranteed to be higher than рЭСЗрЭСЕрЭСГрЭРі
рЭРґ
.
Part of the reason рЭСЗрЭСЕрЭСГрЭРі
рЭРґ
is so far off might be that it is not solved self-consistently, as described
in section 3.5. But there are many other possible errors. The xc-functional might fail to capture
relevant local correlations. There might be relevant physics missing in the Heisenberg model, e.g.
something like eq. (2.5) to take fluctuating moment magnitudes into account. There might be relevant
quantum effects or spin-orbit contributions, which I neglected. It is hard to say where the procedure
goes wrong, when you only have 3 numerical values to debug something involving so many steps and
layers of approximation. For rational studies of critical temperature, you really need hundreds of
materials, or at least dozens, in order to do proper statistics. Or you need to compare something more
informative, excitation spectra.
7.4
Outlook
It would be interesting to extend the theory in chapters 4 and 5 to other magnetic interactions,
for example Dzyaloshinskii-Moriya- or Kitaev interactions, or biquadratic exchange as in [35].
A
particularly useful next step would be to include spin-orbit coupling in the perturbation theory. One
might also try using a Landau-Ginsburg like term to include fluctuations of moment magnitudes in
the model, as done in e.g. [44, 68].
In terms of systems to study, I did not have time to simulate a 2D metal, but an obvious candidates is
the first 2D metallic, ferromagnet, i.e. Fe3GeTe2. It would be very interesting to compare the effect of
different integration volumes and placements in Fe3GeTe2 and CrI3, both bulk and monolayer, as the
former is metallic and the latter an insulating. One could for example test the spectra resulting from
different integration regions against data from inelastic, neutron scattering. This would give a better
understanding of what can and cannot be said through adjustments of the integration regions. For
instance, is it possible to determine exactly where the magnetic moments are located? e.g. on atomic
cores, or in interatomic bonds?. Can you use integration spheres of varying рЭСЯрЭСРto test how localised
the magnetically relevant electrons are?. And what happens when you include several atoms in one
magnetic site? Fe3GeTe2 actually has two equivalent, magnetic atoms close together, which you could
for example capture with a single cylindrical-, ellipsoidal- or spherical integration volume.
Several recent papers have tried to resolve the magnetic interactions into individual orbitals[78, 36].
46
Theory of critical temperatures in two-dimensional itinerant magnets
==PAGE:53==
I think a more fruitful starting point is the spinor density,
ћВрЭСЫ, though the approaches may be com-
plementary. Considering
ћВрЭСЫgives more flexibility, especially for studying delocalised states. Perhaps
one could construct a rigorous, general procedure for determining the extent to which a given set
of magnetic interactions occur between localised or delocalised electrons. Using magnetic response
functions, with site-kernels producing a weighted average of different regions in space, seems like a
reasonable path ahead.
Finally, there are other ways to determine interaction parameters in GPAW, e.g. by fitting to total
energy calculations, so it would be beneficial to follow the example of Bl√Љgel et. al. [46, 95] and
compare the different algorithms on the same systems, to determine their relative strengths and
weaknesses. If the same prediction occurs across numerical schemes, it is more likely to be a physical
effect, rather than a numerical artifact.
7.5
Conclusion
As shown in chapter 2, a central aspect in the problem of predicting critical temperatures for magnetic
ordering is the modelling of spin-wave energies, specifically the ab initio determination of magnon
spectra. In chapter 3, I presented how to calculate magnon spectra from the coupling parameters of a
Heisenberg style spin-model. In chapter 4 I used perturbation theory in the framework of DFT with an
LDA-functional, to derive an expression for the Heisenberg exchange parameters, eq. (4.26). This was
in terms of the non-interacting response function, рЭЬТ+вИТ
рЭРЊрЭСЖ, and a B-field like interaction from the exchange-
correlation effects, both of which can be computed ab initio, using the TDDFT algorithm of Skovhus
and Olsen[76]. In chapter 5, I discretised and Fourier transformed eq. (4.26), to arrive at an expression
that can be computed in the DFT software GPAW, using a pure plane-wave basis. This is the first time
a formula for magnetic interactions based on the magnetic force theorem has been implemented in a
pure plane-wave basis, despite more than 30 years since the first version[48], and continuing efforts in
testing and developing such algorithms[41, 53, 91, 58]. The result is not as accurate as a full TDDFT
algorithm, but it is a lot faster. In chapter 6, I presented details of my implementation, including
the central notion of integration regions, and I presented a convergence study. There, I found that
in a tradeoff between computational time and precision, the main parameters to consider are how
many k-points to use in the simulation grid, and how many reciprocal lattice vectors to include in the
response function calculation. Finally in chapter 7, I compared my algorithm to existing literature
and found an outstanding agreement with the work of Halilov et. al., even though they use a different
method to extract exchange parametersfig. 7.1. The critical temperature results were less promising.
I found clear plateaus in the simulation results as a function of the integration sphere radius. This
strongly suggests that magnetic interactions in transition metals are carried by electrons localised at
the atoms. This ability to determine the real-space localisation of magnetically active electrons, could
help disentangle the complicated interplay between itinerant and localised electrons in magnets. I also
presented proof-of-concept simulations, showing that my algorithm can stability test magnetic states
and simulate more complex crystals than Fe, Co, Ni; including 2D materials.

The first term is a constant. The second shows how each spin experiences the mean spin of its fellows as
a kind of effective B-field, inducing alignment or anti-alignment depending on the coupling parameters.
The last term encodes the interaction between local variations from the mean, hence it includes a lot
of interesting, complicated effects like spin-wave interactions and domain formation.
Without loss of generality, let B = рЭРµeрЭСІ. Because the рЭРµ-field is the only thing to break rotational
symmetry, the mean spin will align with B, regardless of how weak the field is. Hence вЯ®SрЭСЦвЯ©= вЯ®рЭСЖрЭСІ

B.2
Justification for sum to integral mapping
Any real sample you could want to analyse is finite, so there will be some boundary conditions enforcing
a finite, discrete set of allowed magnon modes (or any other modes for that matter). The justification
for replacing sums over q with integrals, as in eq. (B.3), is that the number of modes is so huge, the
discrete spectrum is nigh indistinguishable from a continuum.
To get an idea of the number of modes, consider a box with site length рЭРњ, with cubical unit cells of side
length рЭСО. The allowed modes are standing waves with wavevector q = рЭСЫрЭС•
рЭЬЛ
рЭРњeрЭС•+ рЭСЫрЭС¶
рЭЬЛ
рЭРњeрЭС¶+ рЭСЫрЭСІ
рЭЬЛ
рЭРњeрЭСІwhere
рЭСЫрЭС•, рЭСЫрЭС¶, рЭСЫрЭСІare positive integers. Thus volume
mode =
рЭЬЛ3
рЭРњ3 = рЭЬЛ3
ќ© . The mode with highest spatial frequency
that the atomic lattice can produce is one which changes by рЭЬЛper unit cell, i.e. рЭСЮрЭС•,рЭСЪрЭСОрЭС•= рЭЬЛ
рЭСОand
similarly for рЭСЮрЭС¶, рЭСЮрЭСІ. Even if every atom participates in the movement, they cannot describe a sine-wave
of higher spatial frequency. Thus, k-space volume = рЭЬЛ3
рЭСО3 =
рЭЬЛ3
ќ©рЭСРрЭСТрЭСЩрЭСЩso рЭСБрЭСЪрЭСЬрЭССрЭСТрЭС†= k-space volume
volume/mode =
ќ©
ќ©рЭСРрЭСТрЭСЩрЭСЩ=
рЭСБрЭСОрЭС°рЭСЬрЭСЪрЭС†
atoms per unit cell.
In conclusion; the number of modes is proportional to the number of atoms and typically the same
order of magnitude. For typical samples, we are talking around avogadros number, i.e. 1023. If you are
considering magnons, the magnetic unit cell should be used instead of the atomic, but the conclusion
still stands.

B.3

density scales as 1/ќ© it may be treated as continuous. I now postulate a set of identities for Kronecker

D.3
Magnetic Force Theorem / Full first order energy variation
Here, I present a derivation of the magnetic force theorem from Lichtenstein & Katnselson [48] using
the LDA functional. This amounts to calculating the full energy change between
ћВрЭСЫ0 and
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩpertur-
batively, then noticing that a bunch of terms cancel to first order. At least in LDA. See eq. (4.17) for
ћВрЭСЫ0,
ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩ
We are interested in
ќФрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ= рЭРЄDFT[ ћВрЭСЫрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩ] вИТрЭРЄDFT[ ћВрЭСЫ0]
D.3.1
Explicit рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗ

D.3.5
Magnetic force theorem
Inserting eq. (D.9) into eq. (D.8) and comparing with eq. (D.14), one may observe that рЭЫњрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРґ=
вИТрЭЫњрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩрЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ. This is the essence of the magnetic force theorem. Namely that to first order, relaxation
contributions to рЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРґcancel term-by-term with relaxation contributions to ќФрЭРЄрЭСЖрЭСГ, so relaxation is
strictly a higher order effect. I think the theorem also applies to the rotation рЭЫњu, but this is irrelevant
in the present context.
To compute рЭРљ, we need to go to second order in рЭЫњрЭСҐ, but as discussed in section 4.4.1 рЭЫњрЭСЫ, рЭЫњрЭСЪвИЉрЭТ™(рЭЫњрЭСҐ2).
Thus MFT =вЗТрЭЫњрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗвИЉрЭТ™2(рЭЫњрЭСЫ, рЭЫњрЭСЪ)
рЭСЖрЭС¶рЭСЪрЭСЪрЭСТрЭС°рЭСЯрЭС¶
=======вЗТрЭЫњрЭСЯрЭСТрЭСЩрЭРЄрЭРЈрЭРєрЭСЗвИЉрЭТ™(рЭЬГ). We may thus neglect relaxation

E.1
Symmetry relation
Here, I derive an identity used in chapter 5, which relies on a number of simple symmetry considera-
tions.
Isotropy in k-space means that it is equivalent to sum over G and вИТG. Since the Brillouin zone is
centered at k = 0, the same isotropy allows the replacement вИСq рЭСУ(q) вИТвЖТвИСq рЭСУ(вИТq) at will1. From the
Fourier transform definitions, eqs. (B.5) and (B.6), it follows that if the real-space version of рЭСУis a
real-valued function, then
рЭСУвИТG = рЭСУвИЧ
G
and
рЭСУвИЧ
GGвА≤(q) = рЭСУGвА≤G(q)
Also, one has
рЭСУGGвА≤(вИТq) = рЭСУвИТGвА≤,вИТG(q)

In conclusion, you get the same magnon spectrum whether you use
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q) or рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q). Note that рЭРїрЭЬЗрЭЬИ
1
is Hermitian precisely if рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q) is Hermitian, or equivalently if
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИ(q) is.
E.3
Hermitivity of рЭРљ
The symmetry relations eqs. (E.1) and (E.2) in matrix form are
рЭЬТ+вИТвИЧ= рЭЬТ+вИТрЭСЗ=вЗТрЭЬТ+вИТвА† = рЭЬТ+вИТ
and
рЭРЊрЭЬЗвА† = рЭРЊрЭЬЗвИЧ
where рЭСЗdenotes transpose in G-indices and вА† denotes Hermitian conjugation. It is a general linear
algebra identity that if рЭСО= рЭС£рЭСАрЭС£вИЧ, where рЭСАis a matrix and рЭС£is a vector, then рЭСОвИЧ= рЭС£рЭСАвА†рЭС£вИЧ. Thus,
with reference to eq. (5.9),
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИвИЧ= рЭРµрЭС•рЭСР[рЭРЊрЭЬИрЭЬТ+вИТрЭРЊрЭЬЗ]
вА† рЭРµрЭС•рЭСРвИЧ= рЭРµрЭС•рЭСРрЭРЊрЭЬЗвИЧвА†рЭЬТ+вИТвА†рЭРЊрЭЬИвА† = рЭРµрЭС•рЭСРрЭРЊрЭЬЗрЭЬТ+вИТрЭРЊрЭЬИвИЧрЭРµрЭС•рЭСРвИЧ=
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬИрЭЬЗ
This precisely means that рЭРљis Hermitian. Note that, while рЭРљ(q) is real when there is only one magnetic
site, рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИis only guaranteed to be real for the рЭЬЗ= рЭЬИdiagonal elements.
The difference between
ћЖрЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИand рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИis nothing more than a real-valued, additive constant in the
diagonal, so рЭРљрЭЬЗрЭЬИis also Hermitian.
